<div id="p-lang-btn" class="vector-dropdown mw-portlet mw-portlet-lang"  >
	<input type="checkbox" id="p-lang-btn-checkbox" role="button" aria-haspopup="true" data-event-name="ui.dropdown-p-lang-btn" class="vector-dropdown-checkbox mw-interlanguage-selector" aria-label="Go to an article in another language. Available in 89 languages"   >

<span class="vector-dropdown-label-text">89 languages</span>
	</label>

<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1236090951">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the Hindu goddess. For the Vedic river, see <a href="/wiki/Sarasvati_River" title="Sarasvati River">Sarasvati River</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Saraswati_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Saraswati (disambiguation)">Saraswati (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<li><i><a href="/wiki/Sarasvati_Vandana" class="mw-redirect" title="Sarasvati Vandana">Sarasvatƒ´ VandanƒÅ</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>note 1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label"><a href="/wiki/Hindu_iconography#Symbols_associated_with_individual_devas" title="Hindu iconography">Symbols</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1129693374"><div class="hlist"><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Veena" title="Veena">Veena</a></li><li>books</li><li>rosary</li><li>white lotus</li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Day</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Friday" title="Friday">Friday</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Colour</th><td class="infobox-data"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1129693374"><div class="hlist"><ul><li>White</li><li>yellow</li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label"><a href="/wiki/Vahana" title="Vahana">Mount</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1129693374"><div class="hlist"><ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Hamsa_(bird)" title="Hamsa (bird)">Hamsa</a></i> (white goose or swan)</li><li><a href="/wiki/Peacock" class="mw-redirect" title="Peacock">peacock</a></li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Festivals</th><td class="infobox-data"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1129693374"><div class="hlist"><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Vasant_Panchami" title="Vasant Panchami">Vasant Panchami</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Sarasvati_Puja" class="mw-redirect" title="Sarasvati Puja">Sarasvati Puja</a></li><li>last three (7th,8th &amp; 9th)days of <a href="/wiki/Navaratri" title="Navaratri">Navaratri</a></li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Consort</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Brahma" title="Brahma">Brahma</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Saraswati</b> (<a href="/wiki/Sanskrit_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Sanskrit language">Sanskrit</a>: <span lang="sa">‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä</span>, <a href="/wiki/IAST" class="mw-redirect" title="IAST">IAST</a>: <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Sarasvatƒ´</i></span></i>), also spelled as <b>Sarasvati</b>, is one of the principal goddesses in <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, revered as the <a href="/wiki/Devi" title="Devi">goddess</a> of knowledge, education, learning, arts, speech, poetry, music, purification, language and culture.<sup id="cite_ref-Kinsley1988_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinsley1988-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:9_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Together with the goddesses <a href="/wiki/Lakshmi" title="Lakshmi">Lakshmi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parvati" title="Parvati">Parvati</a>, she forms the trinity, known as the <a href="/wiki/Tridevi" title="Tridevi">Tridevi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:5_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Sarasvati is a pan-Indian deity, venerated not only in Hinduism but also in <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BMA_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BMA-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:5_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>She is one of the prominent goddesses in the <a href="/wiki/Historical_Vedic_religion" title="Historical Vedic religion">Vedic tradition</a> (1500 to 500 BCE) who retains her significance in later Hinduism.<sup id="cite_ref-Kinsley1988_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinsley1988-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a>, her characteristics and attributes are closely connected with the <a href="/wiki/Sarasvati_River" title="Sarasvati River">Sarasvati River</a>, making her one of the earliest examples of a <a href="/wiki/Rivers_in_Hinduism" title="Rivers in Hinduism">river goddess</a> in Indian tradition. As a deity associated with a river, Sarasvati is revered for her dual abilities to purify and to nurture fertility. In later Vedic literature, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Brahmana" title="Brahmana">Brahmanas</a>, Sarasvati is increasingly identified with the Vedic goddess of speech, <a href="/wiki/V%C4%81c" title="VƒÅc">Vac</a>, and eventually, the two merge into the singular goddess known in later tradition. Over time, her connection to the river diminishes, while her association with speech, poetry, music, and culture becomes more prominent. In <a href="/wiki/History_of_Hinduism#Hindu_synthesis_and_Classical_Hinduism_(c._200_BCE_‚Äì_1200_CE)" title="History of Hinduism">classical and medieval Hinduism</a>, Sarasvati is primarily recognized as the goddess of learning, arts and poetic inspiration, and as the inventor of the <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Sanskrit language">Sanskrit language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:9_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kinsley1988_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinsley1988-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> She is linked to the creator god <a href="/wiki/Brahma" title="Brahma">Brahma</a>, either as his consort or creation. In this role, she represents his creative power (<i><a href="/wiki/Shakti" title="Shakti">Shakti</a></i>), giving reality a unique and distinctly human quality. She becomes linked with the dimension of reality characterized by clarity and intellectual order.<sup id="cite_ref-Kinsley1988_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinsley1988-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Within the goddess-oriented <a href="/wiki/Shaktism" title="Shaktism">Shaktism</a> tradition, Sarasvati is a key figure and venerated as the creative aspect of the <a href="/wiki/Mahadevi" title="Mahadevi">Supreme Goddess</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:4_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> She is also significant in certain <a href="/wiki/Vaishnava" class="mw-redirect" title="Vaishnava">Vaishnava</a> traditions, where she serves as one of <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a>'s consorts and assists him in his divine functions.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kinsley1988_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinsley1988-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Despite her associations with these male deities, Sarasvati also stands apart as an independent goddess in the pantheon, worshipped without a consort.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>She is portrayed as a serene woman with a radiant white complexion, dressed in white attire, representing the quality of <i><a href="/wiki/Sattva" title="Sattva">sattva</a></i> (goodness). She has four arms, each holding a symbolic object: a book, a rosary, a water pot, and a musical instrument known as the <a href="/wiki/Veena" title="Veena">veena</a>. Beside her is her mount, either a <i><a href="/wiki/Hamsa_(bird)" title="Hamsa (bird)">hamsa</a></i> (white goose or swan) or a peacock.<sup id="cite_ref-Kinsley1988_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinsley1988-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Hindu temples dedicated to Sarasvati can be found worldwide, with one of the earliest known shrines being <a href="/wiki/Sharada_Peeth" title="Sharada Peeth">Sharada Peeth</a> (6th‚Äì12th centuries CE) in <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmir</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:8_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Sarasvati continues to be widely worshipped across India, particularly on her designated festival day, <a href="/wiki/Vasant_Panchami" title="Vasant Panchami">Vasant Panchami</a> (the fifth day of spring, and also known as Sarasvati Puja and Sarasvati Jayanti in many regions of India), when students honor her as the patron goddess of knowledge and education.<sup id="cite_ref-Kinsley1988_1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinsley1988-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Traditionally, the day is marked by helping young children learn how to write the letters of the alphabet.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, she is venerated in many forms, including the East Asian <a href="/wiki/Benzaiten" title="Benzaiten">Benzaiten</a> (ËæØÊâçÂ§©, "Eloquence Talent Deity").<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tdonaldson_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tdonaldson-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, Sarasvati is revered as the deity responsible for the dissemination of the <a href="/wiki/Tirthankara" title="Tirthankara">Tirthankaras</a>' teachings and sermons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrasad2017192_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrasad2017192-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<dl><dt><div style="font-size: 100%; background-color:#ffd6ad; border-top:0px dashed"><a href="/wiki/Astika" class="mw-redirect" title="Astika">Six Astika schools</a></div></dt></dl>

<ul><li><span class="nowrap"><span class="noviewer" typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Aum_Om_red.svg/16px-Aum_Om_red.svg.png" decoding="async" title="Portal:Hinduism">Hinduism&#32;portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1129693374"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1239400231">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}@media(prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}}@media print{.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:none!important}}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Hinduism" title="Template:Hinduism"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Hinduism" title="Template talk:Hinduism"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:Hinduism" title="Special:EditPage/Template:Hinduism"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Sarasvati is a Sanskrit fusion word of <i>saras</i> (‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç) meaning "pooling water", but also sometimes translated as "speech"; and <i>vati</i> (‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä), meaning "she who possesses". Originally associated with the river or rivers known as Sarasvati, this combination, therefore, means "she who has ponds, lakes, and pooling water" or occasionally "she who possesses speech". It is also a Sanskrit composite word of <i>sarasu-ati</i> (‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•Å+‡§Ö‡§§‡§ø) which means "one with plenty of water".<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jmuir_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jmuir-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The word Sarasvati appears both as a reference to a river and as a significant deity in the <i><a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda</a>.</i> In initial passages, the word refers to the <a href="/wiki/Sarasvati_River" title="Sarasvati River">Sarasvati River</a> and is mentioned as one among several northwestern Indian rivers such as the <a href="/wiki/Drishadvati" class="mw-redirect" title="Drishadvati">Drishadvati</a>. Sarasvati, then, connotes a river deity. In Book 2, the Rigveda describes Sarasvati as the best of mothers, of rivers, of goddesses.<sup id="cite_ref-jmuir_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jmuir-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Her importance grows in the later Vedas composed after the <i>Rigveda</i> as well as in the later <i><a href="/wiki/Brahmana" title="Brahmana">Brahmana</a></i> texts, and the word evolves in its meaning from "waters that purify", to "that which purifies", to "<i>vach</i> (speech) that purifies", to "knowledge that purifies", and ultimately into a spiritual concept of a goddess that embodies knowledge, arts, music, melody, muse, language, rhetoric, eloquence, creative work and anything whose flow purifies the essence and self of a person.<sup id="cite_ref-jmuir_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jmuir-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-emoor_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emoor-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Names_and_epithets">Names and epithets</h3></div>
<p>Sarasvati (Sanskrit: <i>Sarasvatƒ´</i>) is known by many names. Some examples of synonyms for Sarasvati include Sharada (bestower of essence or knowledge),<sup id="cite_ref-Kinsley1988_1-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinsley1988-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Brahmani" class="mw-redirect" title="Brahmani">Brahmani</a> (power of Brahma), Brahmi (goddess of sciences),<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Bharadi (goddess of history), Vani and Vachi (both referring to the flow of music/song, melodious speech, eloquent speaking respectively), Varnesvari (goddess of letters), Kavijihvagravasini (one who dwells on the tongue of poets).<sup id="cite_ref-ebalf_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ebalf-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kinsley1988_1-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinsley1988-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Other names include: <a href="/wiki/Ambika_(Jainism)" title="Ambika (Jainism)">Ambika</a>, Bharati, Chandrika, <a href="/wiki/Devi" title="Devi">Devi</a>, Gomati, Hamsasana, Saudamini, Shvetambara, <a href="/wiki/Subhadra" title="Subhadra">Subhadra</a>, Vaishnavi, Vasudha, Vidya, Vidyarupa, and Vindhyavasini.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Tiruvalluva_Maalai" class="mw-redirect" title="Tiruvalluva Maalai">Tiruvalluva Maalai</a>, a collection of fifty-five Tamil verses praising the <a href="/wiki/Kural_literature" class="mw-redirect" title="Kural literature">Kural literature</a> and its author <a href="/wiki/Valluvar" class="mw-redirect" title="Valluvar">Valluvar</a>, she is referred to as NƒÅmagal and is believed to have composed the second verse.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMohan_Lal,_19924333_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMohan_Lal,_19924333-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamil_Zvelebil,_1975129_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamil_Zvelebil,_1975129-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Outside Nepal and India, she is known in <a href="/wiki/Burmese_language" title="Burmese language">Burmese</a> as <i>Thurathadi</i> (<span title="Burmese-language text"><span lang="my">·Äû·Ä∞·Äõ·Äø·Äê·ÄÆ</span></span>, <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1177148991">.mw-parser-output .IPA-label-small{font-size:85%}.mw-parser-output .references .IPA-label-small,.mw-parser-output .infobox .IPA-label-small,.mw-parser-output .navbox .IPA-label-small{font-size:100%}</style><span class="IPA-label IPA-label-small">pronounced</span> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="my-Latn-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Burmese" title="Help:IPA/Burmese">&#91;Œ∏√πjaÃ∞√∞…ôd√¨&#93;</a></span> or <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="my-Latn-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Burmese" title="Help:IPA/Burmese">&#91;Œ∏√π…πaÃ∞√∞…ôd√¨&#93;</a></span>) or <i><a href="/wiki/Tipitaka" class="mw-redirect" title="Tipitaka">Tipitaka</a> Medaw</i> (<span title="Burmese-language text"><span lang="my">·Äê·Ä≠·Äï·Ä≠·Äã·ÄÄ·Äô·Äö·Ä∫·Äê·Ä±·Ä¨·Ä∫</span></span>, <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1177148991"><span class="IPA-label IPA-label-small">pronounced</span> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="my-Latn-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Burmese" title="Help:IPA/Burmese">&#91;t·∏≠p·∏≠t…ôkaÃ∞<span class="wrap"> </span>m…õÃÄd…îÃÄ&#93;</a></span>), in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a> as <span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Bi√†nc√°itiƒÅn</i></span> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">ËæØÊâçÂ§©</span></span>), in <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a> as <i><a href="/wiki/Benzaiten" title="Benzaiten">Benzaiten</a></i> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">ÂºÅÊâçÂ§©/ÂºÅË≤°Â§©</span></span>) and in <a href="/wiki/Thai_language" title="Thai language">Thai</a> as <i>Suratsawadi</i> (<span title="Thai-language text"><span lang="th">‡∏™‡∏∏‡∏£‡∏±‡∏™‡∏ß‡∏î‡∏µ</span></span>) or <i>Saratsawadi</i> (<span title="Thai-language text"><span lang="th">‡∏™‡∏£‡∏±‡∏™‡∏ß‡∏î‡∏µ</span></span>).<sup id="cite_ref-dk95_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dk95-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Literature">Literature</h2></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, Sarasvati has retained her significance as an important goddess, from the <a href="/wiki/Vedic_period" title="Vedic period">Vedic age</a> up to the present day.<sup id="cite_ref-davidkinsley_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davidkinsley-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> She is praised in the <i><a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a></i> as a water goddess of purification, while in the <i><a href="/wiki/Dharma%C5%9B%C4%81stra" title="Dharma≈õƒÅstra">Dharmashastras</a></i>, Sarasvati is invoked to remind the reader to meditate on virtue, and on the meaning (<a href="/wiki/Artha" title="Artha">artha</a>) of one's actions (<a href="/wiki/Karma" title="Karma">karma</a>).
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="In_Vedic_literature">In Vedic literature</h3></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Rigveda"><i>Rigveda</i></h4></div>
<p>Sarasvati first appears in the <i><a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda</a></i>, the most ancient source of the <a href="/wiki/Historical_Vedic_religion" title="Historical Vedic religion">Vedic religion</a>. Sarawsati holds significant religious and symbolic value in the Rigveda, as a deified entity embodying attributes of abundance and power. Primarily linked with the celestial domain of Waters (Apas) and the formidable Storm Gods (<a href="/wiki/Maruts" title="Maruts">Maruts</a>), this deity forms an integral triadic association alongside the sacrificial goddesses <a href="/wiki/Ila_(Hinduism)" title="Ila (Hinduism)">Ila</a> and Bharati within the pantheon.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Sarasvati is described as a loud and powerful flood who roars like a bull and cannot be controlled.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> She was associated with the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>, indicating that she was seen as descending from heaven to earth.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The goddess is mentioned in many Rigvedic hymns, and has three hymns dedicated to her (6:61 exclusively, and 7:95-96 which she shares with her male counterpart, Sarasvant).<sup id="cite_ref-:6_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In <i>Rigveda</i> 2.41.16 she is called: "Best of mothers, the best of rivers, best of goddesses".<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>As part of the <a href="/wiki/Ap_(water)" title="Ap (water)">Apas</a> (water deities), Sarasvati is associated with wealth, abundance, health, purity and healing.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In Book 10 (10.17) of the <i>Rigveda</i>, Sarasvati is celebrated as a deity of healing and purifying water.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the <i><a href="/wiki/Atharva_Veda" class="mw-redirect" title="Atharva Veda">Atharva Veda</a></i>, her role as a healer and giver of life is also emphasized.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In various sources, including the <i><a href="/wiki/Yajurveda" title="Yajurveda">Yajur Veda</a></i>, she is described as having healed <a href="/wiki/Indra" title="Indra">Indra</a> after he drank too much <a href="/wiki/Soma_(drink)" title="Soma (drink)">Soma</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Sarasvati also governs <i><a href="/wiki/Dhi_(Hindu_thought)" title="Dhi (Hindu thought)">dhƒ´</a></i> (Rigveda 1:3:12c.).<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Dhƒ´</i> is the inspired thought (especially that of the <a href="/wiki/Rishi" title="Rishi">rishis</a>), it is intuition or intelligence ‚Äì especially that associated with poetry and religion. Sarasvati is seen as a deity that can grant <i>dhƒ´</i> (<i>Rigveda</i> 6:49:7c.) if prayed to.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Since speech requires inspired thought, she is also inextricably linked with speech and with the goddess of speech, <a href="/wiki/V%C4%81c" title="VƒÅc">VƒÅc</a>, as well as with cows and motherhood.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Vedic seers compare her to a cow and a mother, and saw themselves as children sucking the milk of <i>dhƒ´</i> from her.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In Book 10 of the <i>Rigveda</i>, she is declared to be the "possessor of knowledge".<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In later sources, like the <i>Yajur Veda</i>, Sarasvati is directly identified with VƒÅc, becoming a deity called Sarasvatƒ´-VƒÅc.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the <i><a href="/wiki/Brahmana" title="Brahmana">Brahmanas</a>,</i> Sarasvati-Vac's role expands, becoming clearly identified with knowledge (which is what is communicated through speech) and as such, she is "the mother of the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a>" as well as the Vedas themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Shatapatha_Brahmana" title="Shatapatha Brahmana">Shatapatha Brahmana</a></i> states that "as all waters meet in the ocean...so all sciences (vidya) unite (ekayanam) in VƒÅc" (14:5:4:11).<sup id="cite_ref-Ludvik_2007,_p._60_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ludvik_2007,_p._60-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <i>Shatapatha Brahmana</i> also presents VƒÅc as a secondary creator deity, having been the first deity created by the creator god <a href="/wiki/Prajapati" title="Prajapati">Prajapati</a>. She is the very instrument by which he created the world, flowing forth from him "like a continuous stream of water" according to the scripture.<sup id="cite_ref-Ludvik_2007,_p._60_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ludvik_2007,_p._60-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This is the basis for the Puranic stories about the relationship between <a href="/wiki/Brahma" title="Brahma">Brahma</a> (identified with Prajapati) and Sarasvati (identified with VƒÅc).<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In other <i>Rigvedic</i> passages, Sarasvati is praised as a mighty and unconquerable protector deity. She is offered praises and compared to a sheltering tree in <i>Rigveda</i> 7.95.5, while in 6:49:7&#160;cd she is said to provide "protection which is difficult to assail."<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In some passages she even takes a fiercesome appearance and is called a "slayer of strangers" who is called on to "guard her devotees against slander".<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Her association with the combative storm gods called Maruts is related to her fierce fighting aspect and they are said to be her companions (at <i>Rigveda</i> 7:96:2c.).<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Like Indra, Sarasvati is also called a slayer of <a href="/wiki/Vritra" title="Vritra">Vritra</a>, the snake like demon of drought who blocks rivers and as such is associated with destruction of enemies and removal of obstacles.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <i>Yajur Veda</i> sees her as being both the mother of Indra (having granted him rebirth through healing) and also as his consort.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In Book 2 of <i><a href="/wiki/Taittir%C4%ABya_Br%C4%81hma%E1%B9%87a" title="Taittirƒ´ya BrƒÅhma·πáa">Taittiriya Brahmana</a></i>, Sarasvati is called "the mother of eloquent speech and melodious music".<sup id="cite_ref-ebalf_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ebalf-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Epic_literature">Epic literature</h3></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_epic" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu epic">Hindu epic</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Mahabharata" title="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</a></i>, "Sarasvati appears above all as a sacred river, along which pilgrimages are made. She is also represented as goddess of speech and knowledge."<sup id="cite_ref-:7_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> She is called "the best of rivers and greatest of streams", and with calm and tranquil waters, in contrast to the mighty torrential Vedic Sarasvati.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Her banks are filled with priests and sages (<a href="/wiki/Rishi" title="Rishi">rishis</a>) who practice asceticism and sacrifices on her banks.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There are numerous depictions of people making pilgrimages to the river to perform sacrifices and bathe in her waters and she often appears in her human form to great seers like <a href="/wiki/Vasishtha" title="Vasishtha">Vasishtha</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Mahabharata</i> also commonly presents her as a goddess of knowledge in her own right and sees Vac as merely a feature of hers.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> She is called the mother of the Vedas in the <i><a href="/wiki/Shanti_Parva" title="Shanti Parva">Shanti Parva</a></i> Book of the epic.<sup id="cite_ref-jmuir_20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jmuir-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Her beauty is also widely commented on by numerous passages and in one passage, the goddess herself states that her knowledge and her beauty arise from gifts made in the sacrifice.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <i>Mahabharata</i> also describes her as the daughter of the creator god Brahma.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Later she is described as the celestial creative symphony who appeared when Brahma created the universe.<sup id="cite_ref-jmuir_20-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jmuir-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the epic <i><a href="/wiki/Ramayana" title="Ramayana">Ramayana</a></i>, when the <a href="/wiki/Rakshasa" title="Rakshasa">rakshasa</a> brothers <a href="/wiki/Ravana" title="Ravana">Ravana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vibhishana" title="Vibhishana">Vibhishana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kumbhakarna" title="Kumbhakarna">Kumbhakarna</a>, performed a penance in order to propitiate Brahma, the creator deity offered each a boon. The devas pleaded with Brahma to not grant Kumbhakarna his boon. Brahma called upon his consort Sarasvati, and instructed her to utter that which the devas desired. She acquiesced, and when the rakshasa spoke to invoke his boon, she entered his mouth, causing him to say, "To sleep for innumerable years, O Lord of Lords, this is my desire!". She then left his form, causing him to reflect upon his misfortune.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Puranic_literature">Puranic literature</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Saraswati_in_the_Indian_Museum,_Kolkata.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Saraswati_in_the_Indian_Museum%2C_Kolkata.jpg/220px-Saraswati_in_the_Indian_Museum%2C_Kolkata.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Saraswati_in_the_Indian_Museum%2C_Kolkata.jpg/330px-Saraswati_in_the_Indian_Museum%2C_Kolkata.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Saraswati_in_the_Indian_Museum%2C_Kolkata.jpg/440px-Saraswati_in_the_Indian_Museum%2C_Kolkata.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="3888" /></a><figcaption>Sarasvati on a Lotus throne playing veena, sandalwood, Mysore, 18th century CE</figcaption></figure>
<p>Sarasvati remains an important figure in the later medieval <a href="/wiki/Puranas" title="Puranas">Puranic literature</a>, where she appears in various myths and stories. Many Puranas relate the myth of her creation by the creator god Brahma and then describe how she became his consort. Sources which describe this myth include <i><a href="/wiki/Markandeya_Purana" title="Markandeya Purana">Markandeya Purana</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Matsya_Purana" title="Matsya Purana">Matsya Purana</a></i> (which contains the most extensive account), <i><a href="/wiki/Vayu_Purana" title="Vayu Purana">Vayu Purana</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Brahmanda_Purana" title="Brahmanda Purana">Brahmanda Purana</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other Puranas give her slightly different roles and see her as the consort of other gods, such as Vishnu. In various Puranas, rites for her worship are given, and she is mainly worshiped for her command over speech, knowledge, and music.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Puranas like the <i>Matsya</i> also contain iconographic descriptions of Sarasvati, which provide the basis for her classic four armed form holding a book (representing the Vedas), <a href="/wiki/Japamala" title="Japamala">mala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Veena" title="Veena">veena</a>, and a water pot while being mounted on a <a href="/wiki/Swan" title="Swan">swan</a> (<a href="/wiki/Hamsa_(bird)" title="Hamsa (bird)">hamsa</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Association_with_Brahma">Association with Brahma</h4></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:12th-century_Brahma_and_Saraswati_at_Shaivism_Hindu_temple_Hoysaleswara_arts_Halebidu_Karnataka_India.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/12th-century_Brahma_and_Saraswati_at_Shaivism_Hindu_temple_Hoysaleswara_arts_Halebidu_Karnataka_India.jpg/220px-12th-century_Brahma_and_Saraswati_at_Shaivism_Hindu_temple_Hoysaleswara_arts_Halebidu_Karnataka_India.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="348" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/12th-century_Brahma_and_Saraswati_at_Shaivism_Hindu_temple_Hoysaleswara_arts_Halebidu_Karnataka_India.jpg/330px-12th-century_Brahma_and_Saraswati_at_Shaivism_Hindu_temple_Hoysaleswara_arts_Halebidu_Karnataka_India.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/12th-century_Brahma_and_Saraswati_at_Shaivism_Hindu_temple_Hoysaleswara_arts_Halebidu_Karnataka_India.jpg/440px-12th-century_Brahma_and_Saraswati_at_Shaivism_Hindu_temple_Hoysaleswara_arts_Halebidu_Karnataka_India.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2548" data-file-height="4032" /></a><figcaption>12th-century Brahma and Sarasvati at Hoysaleswara Temple, Halebidu, Karnataka, India.</figcaption></figure>
<p>According to the <i>Matsya Purana</i>, Brahma generated Sarasvati, here also called by other names like <a href="/wiki/Shatarupa" title="Shatarupa">Shatarupa</a>, Savitri, <a href="/wiki/Gayatri" title="Gayatri">Gayatri</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brahmani_(Matrika)" title="Brahmani (Matrika)">Brahmani</a>, out of himself for the purpose of creation, and mistakes her for his own daughter.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Matsya Purana</i> then describes how Brahma begins to desire her intensely and cannot stop looking at her. Saraswati begins circumambulating him in reverence. Not wishing to keep turning his face to see her, Brahma produced faces on the sides and back of his head. Sarasvati then leapt into the sky and a fifth face emerged from Brahma, looking upwards. Brahma lost his powers as a result of his actions and told his sons to carry out his task, after which he married Sarasvati and they made love for one hundred years.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-www.wisdomlib.org_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-www.wisdomlib.org-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Brahma felt shame and due to his act, the god loses his ascetic power (<a href="/wiki/Tapas_(Indian_religions)" title="Tapas (Indian religions)">tapas</a>) and his sons are left to create the world.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The birth of Sarasvati from the mind of Brahma is also described in the <i><a href="/wiki/Brahmanda_Purana" title="Brahmanda Purana">Brahmanda Purana</a></i> (chapter 43). Sarasvati is tasked to reside on tip of the tongue of all beings, a river on the earth and as a part of Brahma.<sup id="cite_ref-www.wisdomlib.org_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-www.wisdomlib.org-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="In_the_Bhagavata_Purana">In the <i>Bhagavata Purana</i></h4></div>
<p>A legend in the <a href="/wiki/Bhagavata_Purana" title="Bhagavata Purana">Bhagavata Purana</a> describes Sarasvati as originally being one of the three wives of <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a>, along with <a href="/wiki/Lakshmi" title="Lakshmi">Lakshmi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ganga_(goddess)" title="Ganga (goddess)">Ganga</a>. In the midst of a conversation, Sarasvati observed that Ganga playfully kept glancing at Vishnu, behind Lakshmi and her back. Frustrated, Sarasvati launched a furious tirade against Ganga, accusing her of stealing Vishnu's love away from her. When Ganga appealed to her husband to help her, he opted to remain neutral, not wishing to participate in a quarrel between his three wives, whom he loved equally. When Lakshmi attempted to soothe Sarasvati's anger by reasoning with her, the jealous goddess grew angry with her as well, accusing her of disloyalty towards her. She cursed Lakshmi to be born as the <a href="/wiki/Tulasi_in_Hinduism" title="Tulasi in Hinduism">Tulasi</a> plant upon the earth. Ganga, now enraged that Lakshmi had been cursed because she had defended her, cursed Sarasvati that she would be incarnated as a river on earth. Sarasvati issued the same <a href="/wiki/Curse" title="Curse">curse</a> against Ganga, informing her that sinful men would cleanse themselves of their sins with her water. As a result, Vishnu proclaimed that one part of Sarasvati would remain with him, that another would exist as a river on earth, and that another would later become the spouse of Brahma.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Puranic_Narratives_of_Sarasvati‚Äôs_River_Aspect"><span id="Puranic_Narratives_of_Sarasvati.E2.80.99s_River_Aspect"></span>Puranic Narratives of Sarasvati‚Äôs River Aspect</h4></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:1895_CE_Saraswati_on_hamsa_%E0%A4%B8%E0%A4%B0%E0%A4%B8%E0%A5%8D%E0%A4%B5%E0%A4%A4%E0%A5%80_%E0%A4%B9%E0%A4%82%E0%A4%B8_painting_2.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/1895_CE_Saraswati_on_hamsa_%E0%A4%B8%E0%A4%B0%E0%A4%B8%E0%A5%8D%E0%A4%B5%E0%A4%A4%E0%A5%80_%E0%A4%B9%E0%A4%82%E0%A4%B8_painting_2.jpg/220px-1895_CE_Saraswati_on_hamsa_%E0%A4%B8%E0%A4%B0%E0%A4%B8%E0%A5%8D%E0%A4%B5%E0%A4%A4%E0%A5%80_%E0%A4%B9%E0%A4%82%E0%A4%B8_painting_2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="303" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/1895_CE_Saraswati_on_hamsa_%E0%A4%B8%E0%A4%B0%E0%A4%B8%E0%A5%8D%E0%A4%B5%E0%A4%A4%E0%A5%80_%E0%A4%B9%E0%A4%82%E0%A4%B8_painting_2.jpg/330px-1895_CE_Saraswati_on_hamsa_%E0%A4%B8%E0%A4%B0%E0%A4%B8%E0%A5%8D%E0%A4%B5%E0%A4%A4%E0%A5%80_%E0%A4%B9%E0%A4%82%E0%A4%B8_painting_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/1895_CE_Saraswati_on_hamsa_%E0%A4%B8%E0%A4%B0%E0%A4%B8%E0%A5%8D%E0%A4%B5%E0%A4%A4%E0%A5%80_%E0%A4%B9%E0%A4%82%E0%A4%B8_painting_2.jpg/440px-1895_CE_Saraswati_on_hamsa_%E0%A4%B8%E0%A4%B0%E0%A4%B8%E0%A5%8D%E0%A4%B5%E0%A4%A4%E0%A5%80_%E0%A4%B9%E0%A4%82%E0%A4%B8_painting_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2720" data-file-height="3740" /></a><figcaption>Depiction of Sarasvati from a market in Bengal (19th century, pre-1895 CE). The <a href="/wiki/British_Library" title="British Library">British Library</a> curator's summary states, "Sarasvati, the goddess of learning and knowledge, is seated on the banks of a river. Her feet rest on a lotus flower, a palm leaf manuscript to represent the vedas is next to her and she holds a vina. Her mount, a swan, is positioned nearby."</figcaption></figure>
<p>In the <i>Rigveda</i>, Sarasvati is primarily depicted as a river goddess, embodying fertility and purity, and is revered as the personification of the Sarasvati River. Her role as the nurturing, life-giving force of the river is celebrated in hymns, where she is described as "the best of mothers, of rivers, and of goddesses."<sup id="cite_ref-:1_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A Rigvedic prayer also describes her as 'the best of mothers, of rivers and of goddesses'.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, as Sarasvati‚Äôs association with knowledge, speech, and culture grew in prominence through the later Hindu texts, her direct connection with the physical river diminished. Despite this, the Puranas sustain Sarasvati's riverine character by incorporating new narratives that preserve her role as a cosmic river in addition to her expanded identity.<sup id="cite_ref-Kinsley1988_1-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinsley1988-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The story of Sarasvati becoming a river is introduced in the Srishti Khanda of <a href="/wiki/Padma_Purana" title="Padma Purana">Padma Purana</a> as well as in <a href="/wiki/Skanda_Purana" title="Skanda Purana">Skanda Purana</a>. In the Skanda Purana, after the events of the <a href="/wiki/Tarakamaya_War" title="Tarakamaya War">Tarakamaya War</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Deva_(Hinduism)" title="Deva (Hinduism)">devas</a> deposited their arsenal of weapons at the hermitage of <a href="/wiki/Dadhichi" title="Dadhichi">Dadhichi</a>. When they sought the return of these weapons, the sage informed them that he had imbibed all of their power with his penance, and offered his own bones instead, which could serve as the source of new weapons. Despite the objections of the deities, the sage sacrificed himself, and his bones were employed in the manufacture of new arms by <a href="/wiki/Vishvakarma" title="Vishvakarma">Vishvakarma</a>. The sage's son, <a href="/wiki/Pippalada" title="Pippalada">Pippalada</a>, upon hearing these events, sought to wreak his vengeance on the devas by performing a penance. A mare emerged from his right thigh, which in turn gave birth to a fiery man, Vadava, who threatened to be the doom of all of creation. <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a> convinced Vadava that his best course of action would be to swallow the devas one by one, and that he should begin by consuming the primordial water of creation, which was the foremost of both the devas and the asuras. Vadava wished to be accompanied to the source of these waters by a virgin, and so Sarasvati was dispatched for his purpose, despite her reluctance. She took him to <a href="/wiki/Varuna" title="Varuna">Varuna</a>, the god of the ocean, who then consumed the being. For good measure, Sarasvati transformed into a divine river, flowing with five channels into the sea, making the waters sacred.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the Padma Purana, it is stated that there was a terrible battle between the Bhargavas (a group of <a href="/wiki/Brahmin" title="Brahmin">Brahmanas</a>) and the Hehayas (a group of <a href="/wiki/Kshatriya" title="Kshatriya">Kshatriyas</a>). From this, an all-consuming fire called <a href="/wiki/Vadavagni" title="Vadavagni">Vadavagni</a> was born, which threatened to destroy the whole world. In some versions, a sage named Auva created it. <a href="/wiki/Indra" title="Indra">Indra</a>, Vishnu, and the <a href="/wiki/Deva_(Hinduism)" title="Deva (Hinduism)">devas</a> visited Sarasvati, requesting her to deposit the fire in the western ocean, in order to protect the universe.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Sarasvati told Vishnu that she would only agree to assist them if her consort, <a href="/wiki/Brahma" title="Brahma">Brahma</a>, told her to do so. Brahma ordered her to deposit the Vadavagni in the western ocean. Sarasvati agreed, and accompanied by <a href="/wiki/Ganga_(goddess)" title="Ganga (goddess)">Ganga</a>, she left Brahmaloka, and arrived at Sage <a href="/wiki/Uttanka" title="Uttanka">Uttanka's</a> <a href="/wiki/Ashram" title="Ashram">ashrama</a>. There, she met <a href="/wiki/Shiva" title="Shiva">Shiva</a>, who had decided to carry Ganga. He gave the Vadavagni in a pot to Sarasvati, and told her to originate from the <a href="/wiki/Ficus_religiosa" title="Ficus religiosa">plaksha</a> tree. Sarasvati merged with the tree, and transformed into a river. From there, she flowed towards <a href="/wiki/Pushkar" title="Pushkar">Pushkara</a>. Sarasvati continued her journey towards the ocean, and stopped once at Pushkarini, where she redeemed humans from their sins. At last, she reached the end of her journey, and immersed the fire into the ocean.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Shakta_texts">Shakta texts</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Rajamatangi.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Rajamatangi.jpg/220px-Rajamatangi.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="267" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Rajamatangi.jpg/330px-Rajamatangi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/33/Rajamatangi.jpg 2x" data-file-width="377" data-file-height="457" /></a><figcaption>19th century South Indian painting of Raja-Matangi with veena and parrot</figcaption></figure>
<p>Sarasvati is a key figure in the Indian goddess centered traditions which are today known as <a href="/wiki/Shaktism" title="Shaktism">Shaktism</a>. Sarasvati appears in the Puranic <i><a href="/wiki/Devi_Mahatmya" title="Devi Mahatmya">Devi Mahatmya</a></i> (<i>Glory of the <a href="/wiki/Devi" title="Devi">Goddess</a></i>), a central text for Shaktism which was appended to the <i><a href="/wiki/Markandeya_Purana" title="Markandeya Purana">Markandeya Purana</a></i> during the 6th century CE.<sup id="cite_ref-CDC_1988_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_1988-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In this text, she is part of the "triple goddess" (<i><a href="/wiki/Tridevi" title="Tridevi">Tridevi</a></i>) along with <a href="/wiki/Mahakali" title="Mahakali">Mahakali</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mahalakshmi" class="mw-redirect" title="Mahalakshmi">Mahalakshmi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In Shaktism, this trinity (the Shakta response to the male <a href="/wiki/Trimurti" title="Trimurti">trimurti</a> of the other Hindu sects) is a manifestation of <a href="/wiki/Mahadevi" title="Mahadevi">Mahadevi</a>, the supreme goddess (and the highest deity out of which all deities, male or female, are born), which is also known by other names like Adi Parashakti ("Primordial Supreme Power").<sup id="cite_ref-hindufaqs_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hindufaqs-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <i>Devi Mahatmya</i>, this supreme goddess is the primordial creator which is supreme formless (nirguna) consciousness (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Para_Brahman" title="Para Brahman">parabrahman</a>, absolute reality) and the tridevi are her main saguna ("with form", manifest, incarnated) emanations.<sup id="cite_ref-HarperBrown2002p81_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HarperBrown2002p81-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> MahaSarasvati is said to be creative and active principle (which is <a href="/wiki/Rajas" title="Rajas">Rajasic</a>, energetic and active), while Mahalakshmi is the sustainer (<a href="/wiki/Sattva" title="Sattva">sattvic</a>, "goodness") and Mahakali is the destroyer (<a href="/wiki/Tamas_(philosophy)" title="Tamas (philosophy)">tamasic</a>, "darkness").<sup id="cite_ref-HarperBrown2002p81_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HarperBrown2002p81-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In other influential Shakta texts, such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Devi_Bhagavata_Purana" title="Devi Bhagavata Purana">Devi Bhagavata Purana</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Devi_Upanishad" title="Devi Upanishad">Devi Upanishad</a>,</i> Sarasvati (along with all Hindu goddesses) is also said to be a manifestation of the supreme Mahadevi.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Tantra" title="Tantra">Tantric</a> Shakta sources, Sarasvati takes many forms. A key tantric form is <a href="/wiki/Matangi" title="Matangi">Matangi</a>, a deity considered to be the "Tantric Sarasvati". MƒÅta·πÖgƒ´ retains many attributes of Sarasvati, like music and learning, but is also associated with defeating enemies, disease, pollution/impurity, and outcasts (<a href="/wiki/Chandala" title="Chandala">chandalas</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> She is often offered half eaten or leftover food and is green in color. Matangi is also part of the Shakta set of goddesses known as the ten <a href="/wiki/Mahavidya" title="Mahavidya">Mahavidyas</a>.
</p><p>Matangi is important in <a href="/wiki/Shri_Vidya" title="Shri Vidya">Shri Vidya</a> Shaktism, where she is also known as the dark blue Shyamala ("dark in complexion") and is a manifestation of <a href="/wiki/Tripura_Sundari" title="Tripura Sundari">Lalita Tripurasundari</a>'s <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B1%C4%81na" title="J√±ƒÅna">J√±ana</a> Shakti (wisdom power), having arisen out of Lalita's sugarcane bow.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> She is celebrated in the holiday Syamala Navaratri and is seen as Lalita's prime minister. There are various chants and odes (<a href="/wiki/Stotra" title="Stotra">stotras</a>) to this deity, perhaps the most important being the <i>≈örƒ´ ≈öyƒÅmalƒÅ Da·πá·∏çakam</i> by the great Indian Sanskrit poet <a href="/wiki/Kalidasa" title="Kalidasa">Kalidasa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Symbolism_and_iconography">Symbolism and iconography</h2></div>

typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Saraswathidavanagere.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Saraswathidavanagere.JPG/220px-Saraswathidavanagere.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Saraswathidavanagere.JPG/330px-Saraswathidavanagere.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Saraswathidavanagere.JPG/440px-Saraswathidavanagere.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4608" data-file-height="3456" /></a></span></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Iconography" title="Iconography">Iconography</a> of Saraswati: the goddess depicted with her <a href="/wiki/Veena" title="Veena">veena</a>, swan, peacock, crystal <a href="/wiki/Japamala" title="Japamala">japamala</a> and lotus. (Two images: above, a <a href="/wiki/Tile" title="Tile">tile</a> mural in <a href="/wiki/Kerala" title="Kerala">Kerala</a>, below, a sculpture in <a href="/wiki/Cultured_marble" class="mw-redirect" title="Cultured marble">cultured marble</a> in <a href="/wiki/Karnataka" title="Karnataka">Karnataka</a>).</div></div></div></div>
<p>The goddess Sarasvati is often depicted as a beautiful woman dressed in pure white, often seated on a white <a href="/wiki/Nelumbo_nucifera" title="Nelumbo nucifera">lotus</a>, which symbolizes light, knowledge and truth.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> She not only embodies knowledge but also the experience of the highest reality. Her iconography is typically in white themes from dress to flowers to swan ‚Äì the colour symbolizing Sattwa Guna or purity, discrimination for true knowledge, insight and wisdom.<sup id="cite_ref-Kinsley1988_1-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinsley1988-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jholm_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jholm-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Her dhyana mantra describes her to be as white as the moon, clad in a white dress, bedecked in white ornaments, radiating with beauty, holding a book and a pen in her hands (the book represents knowledge).<sup id="cite_ref-Hinduism_101_Saraswati_Symbolism_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hinduism_101_Saraswati_Symbolism-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>She is generally shown to have four arms, but sometimes just two. When shown with four hands, those hands symbolically mirror her husband Brahma's four heads, representing <i>manas</i> (mind, sense), <i>buddhi</i> (intellect, reasoning), <i>citta</i> (imagination, creativity), and <i>ahamkƒÅra</i> (self consciousness, ego).<sup id="cite_ref-gpollock_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gpollock-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Brahma represents the abstract, while she represents action and reality.
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
	<li class="gallerycaption">The variations in iconography of Sarasvati with various musical instruments</li>

			<div class="gallerytext"></div>
		</li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px; height: 150px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Goddess_Saraswati_-_Raja_Ravi_Varma.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Goddess_Saraswati_-_Raja_Ravi_Varma.jpg/83px-Goddess_Saraswati_-_Raja_Ravi_Varma.jpg" decoding="async" width="83" height="120" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Goddess_Saraswati_-_Raja_Ravi_Varma.jpg/125px-Goddess_Saraswati_-_Raja_Ravi_Varma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Goddess_Saraswati_-_Raja_Ravi_Varma.jpg/166px-Goddess_Saraswati_-_Raja_Ravi_Varma.jpg 2x" data-file-width="597" data-file-height="860" /></a></span></div>
