		</li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px; height: 150px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Saraswati_Idol_in_Howrah.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Saraswati_Idol_in_Howrah.jpg/90px-Saraswati_Idol_in_Howrah.jpg" decoding="async" width="90" height="120" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Saraswati_Idol_in_Howrah.jpg/135px-Saraswati_Idol_in_Howrah.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Saraswati_Idol_in_Howrah.jpg/180px-Saraswati_Idol_in_Howrah.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2448" data-file-height="3264" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext"></div>
		</li>
</ul>
<p>The four hands hold items with symbolic meaning ‚Äì a <i>pustaka</i> (book or script), a <i>mƒÅlƒÅ</i> (rosary, garland), a water pot and a musical instrument (<a href="/wiki/V%C4%ABn%C4%81" class="mw-redirect" title="Vƒ´nƒÅ">vƒ´nƒÅ</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Kinsley1988_1-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinsley1988-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The book she holds symbolizes the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a> representing the universal, divine, eternal, and true knowledge as well as all forms of learning. A <a href="/wiki/Hindu_prayer_beads" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu prayer beads">mƒÅlƒÅ</a> of crystals, representing the power of meditation, inner reflection, and spirituality. A pot of water represents the purifying power to separate right from wrong, the clean from the unclean, and essence from the inessential. In some texts, the pot of water is symbolism for <a href="/wiki/Soma_(drink)" title="Soma (drink)">soma</a> ‚Äì the drink that liberates and leads to knowledge.<sup id="cite_ref-Kinsley1988_1-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinsley1988-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The most famous feature on Sarasvati is a musical instrument called a <a href="/wiki/Saraswati_veena" title="Saraswati veena">veena</a>, represents all creative arts and sciences,<sup id="cite_ref-gpollock_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gpollock-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and her holding it symbolizes expressing knowledge that creates harmony.<sup id="cite_ref-Kinsley1988_1-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinsley1988-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Sarasvati is also associated with <i>anurƒÅga</i>, the love for and rhythm of music, which represents all emotions and feelings expressed in speech or music.
</p><p>A hamsa ‚Äì either a <a href="/wiki/Swan" title="Swan">swan</a> or a goose ‚Äì is often shown near her feet. In <a href="/wiki/Hindu_mythology" title="Hindu mythology">Hindu mythology</a>, the hamsa is a sacred bird, which if offered a mixture of milk and water, is said to have a unique ability to separate and drink the milk alone, and leave the water behind. This characteristic of the bird serves as a metaphor for the pursuit of wisdom amidst the complexities of life, the ability to discriminate between good and evil, truth from untruth, essence from the outward show, and the eternal from the evanescent.<sup id="cite_ref-gpollock_84-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gpollock-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Due to her association with the swan, Sarasvati is also referred to as <i>HamsavƒÅhini</i>, which means "she who has a hamsa as her vehicle". The swan is also a symbolism for spiritual perfection, transcendence and <a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">moksha</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jholm_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jholm-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Sometimes a <i>citramekhala</i> (also called <i>mayura</i>, <a href="/wiki/Peacock" class="mw-redirect" title="Peacock">peacock</a>) is shown beside the goddess. The peacock symbolizes colorful splendor, the celebration of dance, and ‚Äì as the devourer of snakes ‚Äì the alchemical ability to transmute the serpent poison of <a href="/wiki/Apasmara" title="Apasmara">self</a> into the radiant plumage of <a href="/wiki/Enlightenment_in_Buddhism" title="Enlightenment in Buddhism">enlightenment</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Forms_and_avatars">Forms and avatars</h2></div>
<p>Many different <a href="/wiki/Avatars" class="mw-redirect" title="Avatars">avatars</a> and forms of Sarasvati have been attested in scriptures.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:1150_CE_Hoysaleswara_temple_Halebidu_Karnataka,_Dancing_Saraswati.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/1150_CE_Hoysaleswara_temple_Halebidu_Karnataka%2C_Dancing_Saraswati.jpg/220px-1150_CE_Hoysaleswara_temple_Halebidu_Karnataka%2C_Dancing_Saraswati.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="405" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/1150_CE_Hoysaleswara_temple_Halebidu_Karnataka%2C_Dancing_Saraswati.jpg/330px-1150_CE_Hoysaleswara_temple_Halebidu_Karnataka%2C_Dancing_Saraswati.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/1150_CE_Hoysaleswara_temple_Halebidu_Karnataka%2C_Dancing_Saraswati.jpg/440px-1150_CE_Hoysaleswara_temple_Halebidu_Karnataka%2C_Dancing_Saraswati.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1558" data-file-height="2866" /></a><figcaption>Dancing Sarasvati with eight-hands (above) is depicted in three panels of the Hoysaleswara temple, Halebid Karnataka (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1150 CE</span>). One of these is shown above. She is in a classical Indian dance posture, and in one of her eight hands she holds a pen, a palm leaf manuscript, a musical instrument and the tools of major arts. The <i>shilpins</i> thus depicted her as the goddess of knowledge and all arts.</figcaption></figure><p>She is venerated as MahaSarasvati in the Kashmir Shakti Peetha, as Vidhya Sarasvati in Basara and Vargal, and as Sharadamba in Sringeri. In some regions, she is known by her twin identities, Savitri and <a href="/wiki/Gayatri" title="Gayatri">Gayatri</a>.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Shaktism" title="Shaktism">Shaktism</a>, she takes her <a href="/wiki/Matrika" class="mw-redirect" title="Matrika">Matrika</a> (mother goddess) avatar as <a href="/wiki/Brahmani" class="mw-redirect" title="Brahmani">Brahmani</a>. Sarasvati is not just the goddess of knowledge and wisdom, but also the Brahmavidya herself, the goddess of the wisdom of ultimate truth. Her Mahavidya form is <a href="/wiki/Matangi" title="Matangi">Matangi</a>.
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Vidya_(philosophy)" title="Vidya (philosophy)">Vidhya</a>, she is the formless concept of wisdom and knowledge in all of its aspects.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gayatri" title="Gayatri">Gayatri</a>, she is the personification of the Vedas</li>
<li>Savitri, She is the personification of purity, the consort of Brahma</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Maha_Sarasvati">Maha Sarasvati</h3></div>
<p>In some regions of India, such as <a href="/wiki/Vindhya_Range" title="Vindhya Range">Vindhya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Odisha" title="Odisha">Odisha</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Bengal" title="West Bengal">West Bengal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Assam" title="Assam">Assam</a>, as well as east Nepal, Sarasvati is part of the <i><a href="/wiki/Devi_Mahatmya" title="Devi Mahatmya">Devi Mahatmya</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Shaktism" title="Shaktism">Shakta</a> mythology, in the <i><a href="/wiki/Tridevi" title="Tridevi">Tridevi</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Mahakali" title="Mahakali">Mahakali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mahalakshmi" class="mw-redirect" title="Mahalakshmi">Mahalakshmi</a> and MahaSarasvati.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This is one of many different Hindu legends that attempt to explain how the Hindu <a href="/wiki/Trimurti" title="Trimurti">trimurti</a> of gods (Brahma, Vishnu and Shiva) and <a href="/wiki/Tridevi" title="Tridevi">goddesses</a> (Sarasvati, Lakshmi and Parvati) came into being. Various <a href="/wiki/Purana" class="mw-redirect" title="Purana">Purana</a> texts offer alternate legends for Maha Sarasvati.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Maha Sarasvati is depicted as eight-armed and is often portrayed holding a Veena whilst sitting on a white lotus flower.
</p><p>
Her <a href="/wiki/Dhyana_in_Hinduism_(Self-knowledge)" class="mw-redirect" title="Dhyana in Hinduism (Self-knowledge)">meditation</a> <a href="/wiki/Shloka" title="Shloka">verse</a> given at the beginning of the fifth chapter the <i>Devi Mahatmya</i> is:</p><blockquote><p>Wielding in her lotus-hands the bell, trident, ploughshare, conch, pestle, discus, bow, and arrow, her lustre is like that of a moon shining in the autumn sky. She is born from the body of <a href="/wiki/Parvati" title="Parvati">Gauri</a> and is the sustaining base of the three worlds. That MahaSarasvati I worship here who destroyed Sumbha and other asuras.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote><p>MahaSarasvati is also part of another legend, the <i>Navshaktis</i> (not to be confused with <i>Navdurgas</i>), or nine forms of Shakti, namely Brahmi, Vaishnavi, Maheshwari, Kaumari, Varahi, Narsimhi, Aindri, Shivdooti, and Chamunda, revered as powerful and dangerous goddesses in eastern India. They have special significance on <i><a href="/wiki/Navaratri" title="Navaratri">Navaratri</a></i> in these regions. All of these are seen ultimately as aspects of a single great Hindu goddess, <a href="/wiki/Durga" title="Durga">Durga</a>, with Maha Sarasvati as one of those nine.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Mahavidya_Nila_Sarasvati">Mahavidya Nila Sarasvati</h3></div>
<p>In Tibet and parts of India, NilaSarasvati is sometimes considered as a form of <a href="/wiki/Mahavidya" title="Mahavidya">Mahavidya</a> <a href="/wiki/Tara_(Devi)" class="mw-redirect" title="Tara (Devi)">Tara</a>. Nila Sarasvati is not much a different deity from traditional Sarasvati, who subsumes her knowledge and creative energy in tantric literature. Though the traditional form of Sarasvati is of calm, compassionate, and peaceful one: Nila Sarasvati is the <i>ugra</i> (angry, violent, destructive) manifestation in one school of Hinduism, while the more common Sarasvati is the <i>saumya</i> (calm, compassionate, productive) manifestation found in most others. In tantric literature of the former, NilaSarasvati has 100&#160;names. There are separate dhyana shlokas and <a href="/wiki/Mantras" class="mw-redirect" title="Mantras">mantras</a> for her worship in <a href="/wiki/Tantrasara" title="Tantrasara">Tantrasara</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-davidkinsley_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davidkinsley-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
She is worshipped in parts of India as an incarnate or incarnation of Goddess Tara but mostly outside India. She is not only worshipped but also been manifested as a form of Goddess Sarasvati.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (February 2019)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Sharada_avatar_in_Kashmir">Sharada avatar in Kashmir</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sharada_Peeth" title="Sharada Peeth">Sharada Peeth</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:DT5248.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/DT5248.jpg/220px-DT5248.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="275" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/DT5248.jpg/330px-DT5248.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/DT5248.jpg/440px-DT5248.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2978" data-file-height="3722" /></a><figcaption>A carved idol of the crowned goddess Sharada from late-9th century Kashmir</figcaption></figure>
<p>The earliest known shrine dedicated to goddess worship in Kashmir is Sharada Peeth (6th‚Äì12th centuries CE), dedicated to the goddess Sharada. It is a <a href="/wiki/Ruins" title="Ruins">ruined</a> <a href="/wiki/Hindu_temple" title="Hindu temple">Hindu temple</a> and ancient centre of learning located in present-day <a href="/wiki/Azad_Kashmir" title="Azad Kashmir">Azad Kashmir</a>. The goddess Sharada worshipped in Sharada Peeth is a tripartite embodiment of the goddess <a href="/wiki/Shakti" title="Shakti">Shakti</a>: Sharada (goddess of learning), Sarasvati (goddess of knowledge), and Vagdevi (goddess of speech, which articulates power).<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kashmiri_Pandit" class="mw-redirect" title="Kashmiri Pandit">Kashmiri Pandits</a> believe the shrine to be the abode of the goddess.<sup id="cite_ref-:8_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In line with the Kashmiri Pandit belief that springs which are the abode of goddesses should not be looked at directly, the shrine contains a stone slab concealing the spring underneath, which they believe to be the spring in which the goddess Sharada revealed herself to the <i><a href="/wiki/Rishi" title="Rishi">rishi</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Shandilya_(Rishi)" title="Shandilya (Rishi)">Shandilya</a>. It advanced the importance of knowledge and education in Kashmiri Pandit culture, which persisted well after Kashmiri Pandits became a minority group in Kashmir.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>As one of the <a href="/wiki/Shakti_Peethas#18_Maha_Shakti_Pithas" class="mw-redirect" title="Shakti Peethas"><i>Maha Shakti Peethas</i></a>, Hindus believe that it represents the spiritual location of the goddess <a href="/wiki/Sati_(Hindu_goddess)" title="Sati (Hindu goddess)">Sati's</a> fallen right hand. Sharada Peeth is one of the three holiest sites of pilgrimage for <a href="/wiki/Kashmiri_Pandits" title="Kashmiri Pandits">Kashmiri Pandits</a>, alongside the <a href="/wiki/Martand_Sun_Temple" title="Martand Sun Temple">Martand Sun Temple</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Amarnath_Temple" title="Amarnath Temple">Amarnath Temple</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Worship">Worship</h2></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Temples">Temples</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1237032888/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Saraswati_Mandir_Temple.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Saraswati_Mandir_Temple.jpg/220px-Saraswati_Mandir_Temple.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="166" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Saraswati_Mandir_Temple.jpg/330px-Saraswati_Mandir_Temple.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Saraswati_Mandir_Temple.jpg/440px-Saraswati_Mandir_Temple.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3976" data-file-height="2998" /></a></span></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Basara_Temple_view.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Basara_Temple_view.jpg/220px-Basara_Temple_view.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Basara_Temple_view.jpg/330px-Basara_Temple_view.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Basara_Temple_view.jpg/440px-Basara_Temple_view.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="375" /></a></span></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Saraswati temple at <a href="/wiki/Pilani" title="Pilani">Pilani</a> in North Indian style (above), and South Indian style (below). Her temples, like her iconography, often resonate in white themes.</div></div></div></div>
<p>There are many Hindu temples dedicated to Sarasvati around the world. Some notable temples include
</p><p>‚Ä¢<a href="/wiki/Gnana_Saraswati_Temple,_Basar" class="mw-redirect" title="Gnana Saraswati Temple, Basar">Gnana Sarasvati Temple</a> in <a href="/wiki/Basar,_Andhra_Pradesh" class="mw-redirect" title="Basar, Andhra Pradesh">Basar</a> on the banks of the River <a href="/wiki/Godavari" class="mw-redirect" title="Godavari">Godavari</a>,
</p><p>‚Ä¢one the 18 <a href="/wiki/Shaktipeeth" class="mw-redirect" title="Shaktipeeth">Mahasaktipeethas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sharada_Peeth" title="Sharada Peeth">Sharada Peeth</a> is dedicated to goddess sarasvati
</p><p>‚Ä¢<a href="/wiki/Savitri_Mata_Mandir" title="Savitri Mata Mandir">Savitri Mata Mandir</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pushkar" title="Pushkar">Pushkar</a>
</p><p>‚Ä¢<a href="/wiki/Saraswati_Temple,_Wargal" class="mw-redirect" title="Saraswati Temple, Wargal">Wargal Sarasvati</a> and Shri Sarasvati Kshetramu temples in Medak, <a href="/wiki/Telangana" title="Telangana">Telangana</a>.
</p><p>‚Ä¢In <a href="/wiki/Karnataka" title="Karnataka">Karnataka</a>, one of many Sarasvati/Sharada pilgrimage spots is <a href="/wiki/Shringeri_Sharadamba_Temple" class="mw-redirect" title="Shringeri Sharadamba Temple">Shringeri Sharadamba Temple</a>.
</p><p>‚Ä¢ <a href="/wiki/Kollur_Mookambika_Temple" title="Kollur Mookambika Temple">Kollur Mookambika Temple</a> situated in kollur, Karnataka.
</p><p>‚Ä¢ <a href="/wiki/Chottanikkara_Temple" title="Chottanikkara Temple">Chottanikkara Temple</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Ernakulam" title="Ernakulam">Ernakulam</a> district of <a href="/wiki/Kerala" title="Kerala">Kerala</a>
</p><p>‚Ä¢<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.avanamcodesaraswathi.com/">Avanamcode Saraswati temple</a>, situated near <a href="/wiki/Nedumbasserry_International_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="Nedumbasserry International Airport">Nedumbasserry International Airport</a>, Kerala is one among 108 durgalayas temple in Kerala
</p><p>‚Ä¢ <a href="/wiki/Dakshina_Mookambika_Temple,_North_Paravur" title="Dakshina Mookambika Temple, North Paravur">Dakshina Mookambika Temple, North Paravur</a>
</p><p>‚Ä¢<a href="/wiki/Panachikkadu_Temple" title="Panachikkadu Temple">Panachikkadu saraswati Temple</a>
</p><p>‚Ä¢<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://bet.org.in/instutions/sharda-peeth-pilani/">Sarada peeth, pilani, rajasthan</a>
</p><p>‚Ä¢<a href="/wiki/Koothanur_Maha_Saraswathi_Temple" title="Koothanur Maha Saraswathi Temple">Koothanur Maha Saraswathi Temple</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Koothanur" title="Koothanur">Koothanur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Nadu" title="Tamil Nadu">Tamil Nadu</a>
</p><p>‚Ä¢<a href="/wiki/Maa_Sharda_Mandir,_Maihar" title="Maa Sharda Mandir, Maihar">Maa Sharda Mandir, Maihar</a> in <a href="/wiki/Madhya_Pradesh" title="Madhya Pradesh">Madhya Pradesh</a>
</p><p>‚Ä¢In her as<a href="/wiki/Brahmani_(Matrika)" title="Brahmani (Matrika)">Brahmani</a>, additional Sarasvati temples can be found throughout Gujarat, Himachal Pradesh, Rajasthan, and Uttar Pradesh.
</p><p>‚Ä¢<a href="/w/index.php?title=Jnaneswari_peeth&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jnaneswari peeth (page does not exist)">Jnaneswari peeth</a> in Karki village of coastal Karnataka also houses a temple dedicated to Sarasvati, where she is known as Jnaneshwari.
</p><p>‚Ä¢<a href="/wiki/Sarala_Temple" title="Sarala Temple">Sarala Temple</a> in <a href="/wiki/Jagatsinghpur" title="Jagatsinghpur">Jagatsinghpur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Odisha" title="Odisha">Odisha</a>
</p><p>‚Ä¢<a href="/wiki/Pura_Taman_Saraswati" title="Pura Taman Saraswati">Pura Taman Saraswati</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Balinese_temple" title="Balinese temple">Balinese hindu temple</a> dedicated to saraswati
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Festivals_and_pujas">Festivals and pujas</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Saraswati_Puja" class="mw-redirect" title="Saraswati Puja">Saraswati Puja</a></div>
<p>One of the most famous festivals associated with Goddess Sarasvati is the Hindu festival of Vasant Panchami. Celebrated on the 5th day in the Hindu calendar month of <a href="/wiki/Magha_(month)" title="Magha (month)">Magha</a>, it is also known as <a href="/wiki/Saraswati_Puja" class="mw-redirect" title="Saraswati Puja">Sarasvati Puja</a> and Sarasvati Jayanti in India.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="In_south_India">In south India</h4></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Kerala" title="Kerala">Kerala</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Nadu" title="Tamil Nadu">Tamil Nadu</a>, the last three days of the <a href="/wiki/Navaratri" title="Navaratri">Navaratri</a> festival, i.e., Ashtami, Navami, and Dashami, are celebrated as Sarasvati Puja.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The celebrations start with the <i>Puja Vypu</i> (Placing for Worship). It consists of placing the books for puja on the Ashtami day. It may be in one's own house, in the local nursery school run by traditional teachers, or in the local temple. The books are taken out for reading, after worship, only on the morning of the third day (Vijaya Dashami). It is called <i>Puja Eduppu</i> (Taking [from] Puja). On the Vijaya Dashami day, Kerala and Tamil Nadu celebrate the <i>E·∏ªuthiniruthu</i> or "Initiation of writing" for children, before they are admitted to nursery schools. This is also called <i>Vidyarambham</i>. The child is often ritually taught to write for the first time on rice spread in a plate with their index finger, guided by an elder of the family, or by a teacher.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="In_east_and_northeast_India">In east and northeast India</h4></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Saraswati_puja_festival6.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Saraswati_puja_festival6.jpg/220px-Saraswati_puja_festival6.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="391" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Saraswati_puja_festival6.jpg/330px-Saraswati_puja_festival6.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Saraswati_puja_festival6.jpg/440px-Saraswati_puja_festival6.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2250" data-file-height="4000" /></a><figcaption>Sarasvati Puja, <a href="/wiki/Chandannagar" title="Chandannagar">Chandannagar</a>, West Bengal</figcaption></figure>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Assam" title="Assam">Assam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Odisha" title="Odisha">Odisha</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Bengal" title="West Bengal">West Bengal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tripura" title="Tripura">Tripura</a>, Goddess Sarasvati is worshipped on Vasant Panchami, a Hindu festival celebrated every year on the 5th day in the Hindu calendar month of <a href="/wiki/Magha_(month)" title="Magha (month)">Magha</a> (about February). Hindus celebrate this festival in temples, homes and educational institutes alike.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="In_north,_west,_and_central_India"><span id="In_north.2C_west.2C_and_central_India"></span>In north, west, and central India</h4></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jharkhand" title="Jharkhand">Jharkhand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vasant_Panchami" title="Vasant Panchami">Vasant Panchami</a> is commonly known as <a href="/wiki/Saraswati_Puja" class="mw-redirect" title="Saraswati Puja">Sarasvati Puja</a>. On this day, Goddess Sarasvati is worshipped in schools, colleges, educational institutes as well as in institutes associated with music and dance. Cultural programmes are also organised in schools and institutes on this day. People especially students worship Goddess Sarasvati also in pandals (a tent made up of colourful clothes, decorated with lights and other decorative items). In these states, on the occasion of Sarasvati Puja, Goddess Sarasvati is worshipped in the form of idol, made up of soil. On Sarasvati Puja, the idol is worshipped by people and <a href="/wiki/Prasad" class="mw-redirect" title="Prasad">prasad</a> is distributed among the devotees after <a href="/wiki/Puja_(Hinduism)" title="Puja (Hinduism)">puja</a>. Prasad mainly consists of <a href="/wiki/Boondi" title="Boondi">boondi</a> (<i>motichoor</i>), pieces of carrot, peas and Indian plum (ber). On the next day or any day depending on religious condition, the idol is immersed in a pond (known as Murti Visarjan or Pratima Visarjan) after performing a Havana (immolation), with full joy and fun, playing with <a href="/wiki/Abir" class="mw-redirect" title="Abir">abir</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gulal" title="Gulal">gulal</a>. After Pratima Visarjan, members involved in the organisation of puja ceremony eat <a href="/wiki/Khichdi" class="mw-redirect" title="Khichdi">khichdi</a> together.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Goa" title="Goa">Goa</a>, Sarasvati Puja starts with Sarasvati Avahan on Maha Saptami and ends on Vijayadashami with Sarasvati Udasan or Visarjan.<sup id="cite_ref-goa_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-goa-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2018, the <a href="/wiki/Haryana" title="Haryana">Haryana</a> government launched and sponsored the annual National Sarasvati Mahotsav in its state named after Sarasvati.<sup id="cite_ref-maho1_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-maho1-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="In_Indonesia">In Indonesia</h4></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Pura_Taman_Saraswati_(Ubud,_Bali,_Indonesia).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Pura_Taman_Saraswati_%28Ubud%2C_Bali%2C_Indonesia%29.jpg/220px-Pura_Taman_Saraswati_%28Ubud%2C_Bali%2C_Indonesia%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="90" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Pura_Taman_Saraswati_%28Ubud%2C_Bali%2C_Indonesia%29.jpg/330px-Pura_Taman_Saraswati_%28Ubud%2C_Bali%2C_Indonesia%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Pura_Taman_Saraswati_%28Ubud%2C_Bali%2C_Indonesia%29.jpg/440px-Pura_Taman_Saraswati_%28Ubud%2C_Bali%2C_Indonesia%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6223" data-file-height="2544" /></a><figcaption>Pura Taman Sarasvati, <a href="/wiki/Bali" title="Bali">Bali</a></figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Watu_Gunung" title="Watu Gunung">Watugunung</a>, the last day of the <a href="/wiki/Pawukon_calendar" title="Pawukon calendar">pawukon calendar</a>, is devoted to Sarasvati, goddess of learning. Although it is devoted to books, reading is not allowed. The fourth day of the year is called Pagerwesi, meaning "iron fence". It commemorates a battle between good and evil.<sup id="cite_ref-Eiseman184185_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eiseman184185-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Sarasvati is an important goddess in Balinese Hinduism. She shares the same attributes and iconography as Sarasvati in Hindu literature of India ‚Äì in both places, she is the goddess of knowledge, creative arts, wisdom, language, learning and purity. In <a href="/wiki/Bali" title="Bali">Bali</a>, she is celebrated on Sarasvati day, one of the main festivals for Hindus in Indonesia.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The day marks the close of 210&#160;day year in the Pawukon calendar.<sup id="cite_ref-msz_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-msz-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>On Sarasvati day, people make offerings in the form of flowers in temples and to sacred texts. The day after Sarasvati day, is <i>Banyu Pinaruh</i>, a day of cleansing. On this day, Hindus of Bali go to the sea, sacred waterfalls or river spots, offer prayers to Sarasvati, and then rinse themselves in that water in the morning. Then they prepare a feast, such as the traditional <i>bebek betutu</i> and <i>nasi kuning</i>, that they share.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The Sarasvati Day festival has a long history in Bali.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It has become more widespread in Hindu community of Indonesia in recent decades, and it is celebrated with theatre and dance performance.<sup id="cite_ref-msz_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-msz-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Southeast_Asia">Southeast Asia</h4></div>
<p>Sarasvati was honoured with invocations among the Hindus of <a href="/wiki/Angkor" title="Angkor">Angkorian</a> <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-oww_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oww-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> She and Brahma are referred to in Cambodian <a href="/wiki/Epigraphy" title="Epigraphy">epigraphy</a> from the 7th century onwards, and she is praised by Khmer poets for being the goddess of eloquence, writing, and music. More offerings were made to her than to her husband Brahma. She is also referred to as <i>Vagisvari</i> and <i>Bharati</i> in the Khmer literature of the era of <a href="/wiki/Yasovarman_I" title="Yasovarman I">Yasovarman</a>, Hindu king of the <a href="/wiki/Khmer_Empire" title="Khmer Empire">Khmer Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-oww_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oww-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="In_Buddhism">In Buddhism</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Thurathadi,_Insein_Kyaukdawgyi_Buddha.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Thurathadi%2C_Insein_Kyaukdawgyi_Buddha.JPG/220px-Thurathadi%2C_Insein_Kyaukdawgyi_Buddha.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Thurathadi%2C_Insein_Kyaukdawgyi_Buddha.JPG/330px-Thurathadi%2C_Insein_Kyaukdawgyi_Buddha.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Thurathadi%2C_Insein_Kyaukdawgyi_Buddha.JPG/440px-Thurathadi%2C_Insein_Kyaukdawgyi_Buddha.JPG 2x" data-file-width="787" data-file-height="1049" /></a><figcaption>Statue of Thurathadi at <a href="/wiki/Kyauktawgyi_Buddha_Temple_(Yangon)" title="Kyauktawgyi Buddha Temple (Yangon)">Kyauktawgyi Buddha Temple (Yangon)</a></figcaption></figure><p>
In <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, Sarasvati became a prominent deity which retained many of her Vedic associations, such as speech, texts, knowledge, healing and protection. She also became known as the consort of <a href="/wiki/Manjushri" title="Manjushri">Manjushri</a>, the bodhisattva of wisdom (<a href="/wiki/Praj%C3%B1%C4%81_(Buddhism)" title="Praj√±ƒÅ (Buddhism)">praj√±a</a>). According to Miranda Shaw's <i>Buddhist Goddesses of India</i>:</p><blockquote><p>Sarasvati's association with the intellectual sphere assured that she would find favor among Buddhists, who highly value wisdom and its servants: mental clarity, reasoning ability, memorization, and oratorical skill. Sarasvati thus has an affinity with <a href="/wiki/Praj%C3%B1%C4%81p%C4%81ramit%C4%81_Devi" class="mw-redirect" title="Praj√±ƒÅpƒÅramitƒÅ Devi">Praj√±aparamita</a>, the goddess of perfect wisdom. They may be in voked by the same mantra, reflecting the kinship between the wisdom goddess and the patroness of learning.<sup id="cite_ref-Shaw_2006,_p._236_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shaw_2006,_p._236-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> </p></blockquote><p>Shaw lists various epithets for Sarasvati used by Buddhist source including: "Emanation of Vishnu," "Gandharva Maiden," "Swan Child," "Daughter of Brahma", "Lady of the Lake", "Sister of the Moon", "Goddess of Speech", "Divine Lady Who Empowers Enlightened Speech", "Goddess Rich with the Power of Adamantine Speech", "Bestower of Understanding", "Goddess of Knowledge", and "Wisdom Goddess."<sup id="cite_ref-Shaw_2006,_p._236_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shaw_2006,_p._236-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
According to Shaw, Buddhist depictions of Sarasvati are influenced by Hindu ones. A popular depiction is called "Lady of the Adamantine Lute" (Vajravina) which is described by Shaw as.</p><blockquote><p>a white, two-armed epiphany in which she plays her supernal lute, or vina. The instrument is made of lapis lazuli and has a thousand strings capable of eliciting every musical note. Sarasvati's melodies pervade the universe and delight all types of beings in accordance with whatever is most pleasing to their ears. She sits with ankles crossed and knees raised in a distinctive posture suitable for balancing a musical instrument.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> </p></blockquote><p>Sarasvati's earliest appearance in a Buddhist text is in the 1st century CE <a href="/wiki/Mahayana" title="Mahayana">Mahayana</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Golden_Light_Sutra" title="Golden Light Sutra">Golden Light Sutra</a></i> (of which there are different versions / translations). This text is first attested in a Chinese translation in 417&#160;CE and includes an entire chapter devoted to the goddess, which is our best source for the earliest Buddhist depictions of Sarasvati.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="In_the_Golden_Light_Sutra">In the <i>Golden Light Sutra</i></h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Benzaiten_Surrounded_by_the_Goddesses_Kariteimo_and_Kenrochijin_and_Two_Divine_Generals,_from_Kichijoten_shrine,_c._1212.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Benzaiten_Surrounded_by_the_Goddesses_Kariteimo_and_Kenrochijin_and_Two_Divine_Generals%2C_from_Kichijoten_shrine%2C_c._1212.jpg/220px-Benzaiten_Surrounded_by_the_Goddesses_Kariteimo_and_Kenrochijin_and_Two_Divine_Generals%2C_from_Kichijoten_shrine%2C_c._1212.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="358" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Benzaiten_Surrounded_by_the_Goddesses_Kariteimo_and_Kenrochijin_and_Two_Divine_Generals%2C_from_Kichijoten_shrine%2C_c._1212.jpg/330px-Benzaiten_Surrounded_by_the_Goddesses_Kariteimo_and_Kenrochijin_and_Two_Divine_Generals%2C_from_Kichijoten_shrine%2C_c._1212.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Benzaiten_Surrounded_by_the_Goddesses_Kariteimo_and_Kenrochijin_and_Two_Divine_Generals%2C_from_Kichijoten_shrine%2C_c._1212.jpg/440px-Benzaiten_Surrounded_by_the_Goddesses_Kariteimo_and_Kenrochijin_and_Two_Divine_Generals%2C_from_Kichijoten_shrine%2C_c._1212.jpg 2x" data-file-width="955" data-file-height="1555" /></a><figcaption>A Japanese depiction of Sarasvati as a protector deity with eight arms holding various weapons (c. 1212), University Art Museum, <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_University_of_the_Arts" title="Tokyo University of the Arts">Tokyo University of the Arts</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>In the <i>Golden Light Sutra</i> (<i>Suvar·πáaprabhƒÅsa S≈´tra</i>), Sarasvati appears and pays homage to the Buddha. As Shaw writes, she then "promises that she will grace the preachers of the scripture with eloquence, oratorical power, perfect memory, inconceivable knowledge, penetrating wisdom, illumination, skill in liberating others, scholarly expertise in every field, proficiency in all the arts, merit, prosperity, and long life."<sup id="cite_ref-Shaw_2006,_p._237_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shaw_2006,_p._237-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Sarasvati's chapter in the <i>Golden Light Sutra</i> presents three main aspects of the goddess. First, it presents her as a goddess of eloquence and speech, then it presents her as a healing goddess who teaches a ritual which includes a medicinal bath, finally it presents Sarasvati as a goddess of protection and war.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Ludvik mentions that the earliest version of the <i>Golden Light Sutra</i> (the translation by Dharmaksema) actually only includes the first depiction.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The early Chinese Buddhist translators chose to translate her name as "great eloquence deity" (Â§ßËæØÂ§©) the later translations by Yijing use "Eloquence Talent Goddess" (Biancai tiannu), though phonetic translations were also applied (e.g. Yijing's "mohetipi suoluosuobodi").<sup id="cite_ref-:2_112-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the <i>Golden Light Sutra</i>, Sarasvati is closely associated with <a href="/wiki/Eloquence" title="Eloquence">eloquence</a>, as well as with the closely connected virtues of <a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">memory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Sarasvati is also said to help monks memorize the Buddhist <a href="/wiki/Sutra" title="Sutra">sutras</a> and to guide them so they will not make mistakes in memorizing them or forget them later. She will also help those who have incomplete manuscripts to regain the lost letters or words. She also teaches a <a href="/wiki/Dharani" title="Dharani">dharani</a> (a long mantra-like recitation) to improve memory.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>113<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <i>Golden Light</i> goes as far as to claim that Sarasvati can provide the wisdom to understand all the Buddhist teachings and skillful means (<a href="/wiki/Upaya" title="Upaya">upaya</a>) so that one may swiftly attain Buddhahood.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>114<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In some versions of the <i>Golden Light Sutra</i>, such as Yijing's, the goddess then teaches an <a href="/wiki/Apotropaic_magic" title="Apotropaic magic">apotropaic</a> ritual that can combat disease, bad dreams, war, calamities and all sorts of negative things. It includes bathing in a bath with numerous herbs that has been infused with a <a href="/wiki/Dharani" title="Dharani">dharani</a> spell. This passage contains much information on ancient <a href="/wiki/Materia_medica" title="Materia medica">materia medica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Herbal_medicine" title="Herbal medicine">herbology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>115<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Ludvik adds that this may be connected to her role as healer of Indra in the <i><a href="/wiki/Yajurveda" title="Yajurveda">Yajur Veda</a></i> and to ancient Indian bathing rites.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the latter part of the <i>Golden Light</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s Sarasvati chapter, she is praised as a protector goddess by the Brahman Kaundinya. This section also teaches a dharani and a ritual to invoke the goddess and receive her blessings in order to obtain knowledge.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>117<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In latter sections of Kaundinya's praise, she is described as an eight armed goddess and compared to a lion. The text also states that is some recites these praises, "one obtains all desires, wealth and grain, and one gains splendid, noble success."<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The poem describes Sarasvati as one who "has sovereignty in the world", and states that she fights in battlefields and is always victorious.<sup id="cite_ref-Harivamsa2_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harivamsa2-120"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>119<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The hymn then describes Sarasvati's warlike eight-armed form. She carries eight weapons in each hand ‚Äì a bow, arrow, sword, spear, axe, <a href="/wiki/Vajra" title="Vajra">vajra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chakram" title="Chakram">iron wheel</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pasha_(Hinduism)" title="Pasha (Hinduism)">noose</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>120<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Kaudinya's hymn to Sarasvati in Yijing's translation is derived from the <i>ƒÄryƒÅstava</i> ("praise of she who is noble"), a hymn uttered by Vishnu to the goddess Nidra (lit. "Sleep", one of the names applied to <a href="/wiki/Durga" title="Durga">Durga</a>) found in the <i><a href="/wiki/Harivamsa" class="mw-redirect" title="Harivamsa">Harivamsha</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>121<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As the <i>Golden Light Sutra</i> is often concerned with the protection of the state, it is not surprising that the fierce, weapon-wielding Durga, who was widely worshiped by rulers and warriors alike for success in battle, provides the model for the appearance assumed by Sarasvati, characterized as a protectress of the Buddhist Dharma.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>122<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Faure" title="Bernard Faure">Bernard Faure</a> argues that the emergence of a martial Sarasvatƒ´ may have been influenced by the fact that "VƒÅc, the Vedic goddess of speech, had already displayed martial characteristics. [...] Already in the <i>Vedas</i>, it is said that she destroys the enemies of the gods, the asuras. Admittedly, later sources seem to omit or downplay that aspect of her powers, but this does not mean that its importance in religious practice was lost."<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Other_Indian_Mahayana_sources">Other Indian Mahayana sources</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Vajra_Sarada_-_Basalt_-_Pala_Period_Circa_8th_Century_AD_-_Nalanda_-_Archaeological_Museum_-_Nalanda_-_Bihar_-_Indian_Buddhist_Art_-_Exhibition_-_Indian_Museum_-_Kolkata_2012-12-21_2353.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Vajra_Sarada_-_Basalt_-_Pala_Period_Circa_8th_Century_AD_-_Nalanda_-_Archaeological_Museum_-_Nalanda_-_Bihar_-_Indian_Buddhist_Art_-_Exhibition_-_Indian_Museum_-_Kolkata_2012-12-21_2353.JPG/220px-thumbnail.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="265" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Vajra_Sarada_-_Basalt_-_Pala_Period_Circa_8th_Century_AD_-_Nalanda_-_Archaeological_Museum_-_Nalanda_-_Bihar_-_Indian_Buddhist_Art_-_Exhibition_-_Indian_Museum_-_Kolkata_2012-12-21_2353.JPG/330px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Vajra_Sarada_-_Basalt_-_Pala_Period_Circa_8th_Century_AD_-_Nalanda_-_Archaeological_Museum_-_Nalanda_-_Bihar_-_Indian_Buddhist_Art_-_Exhibition_-_Indian_Museum_-_Kolkata_2012-12-21_2353.JPG/440px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2689" data-file-height="3237" /></a><figcaption>A statue of Vajra≈õƒÅradƒÅ, a classic Buddhist form of Sarasvati, <a href="/wiki/Pala_Empire" title="Pala Empire">Pala Period</a> (8th century CE), <a href="/wiki/Indian_Museum" title="Indian Museum">Indian Museum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kolkata" title="Kolkata">Kolkata</a></figcaption></figure><p>
In some later <a href="/wiki/Mahayana" title="Mahayana">Mahayana Buddhist</a> sources like the <i>SƒÅdhanamƒÅlƒÅ</i> (a 5th-century collection of ritual texts), Sarasvati is symbolically represented in a way which is similar to Hindu iconography.<sup id="cite_ref-tdonaldson_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tdonaldson-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The description of the deity (here called MahƒÅsarasvatƒ´) is as follows:</p><blockquote><p>The worshipper should think himself as goddess MahƒÅsarasvatƒ´, who is resplendent like the autumn moon, rests on the moon over the white lotus, shows the <i>varada-mudrƒÅ</i> in her right hand, and carries in the left the white lotus with its stem. She has a smiling countenance, is extremely compassionate, wears garments decorated with white sandal-flowers. Her bosom is decorated with the pearl-necklace, and she is decked in many ornaments; she appears a maiden of twelve years, and her bosom is uneven with half-developed breasts like flower-buds; she illumines the three worlds with the immeasurable light that radiates from her body.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>124<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote><p>In the <i>SƒÅdhanamƒÅlƒÅ,</i> the mantra of Sarasvati is: o·πÉ hrƒ´·∏• mahƒÅmƒÅyƒÅ·πÖge mahƒÅsarasvatyai nama·∏•
</p><p>The <i>SƒÅdhanamƒÅlƒÅ</i> also depicts other forms of Sarasvati, including Vajravƒ´·πáƒÅ Sarasvatƒ´ (similar to MahƒÅsarasvatƒ´ except she carries a <a href="/wiki/Veena" title="Veena">veena</a>), Vajra≈õƒÅradƒÅ Sarasvatƒ´ (who has three eyes, sits on a white lotus, her head is decorated by a crescent and holds a book and a lotus), Vajrasarasvatƒ´ (has six hands and three heads with brown hair rising upwards), and ƒÄryasarasvatƒ´ (sixteen-year-old girl carrying the Praj√±apramita sutra and a lotus).<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>125<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <i><a href="/wiki/K%C4%81ra%E1%B9%87%E1%B8%8Davy%C5%ABha_S%C5%ABtra" title="KƒÅra·πá·∏çavy≈´ha S≈´tra">KƒÅra·πá·∏çavy≈´ha S≈´tra</a></i> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 4th century ‚Äì 5th century CE<i>), Sarasvati was born from the eyetooth of <a href="/wiki/Avalokite%C5%9Bvara" title="Avalokite≈õvara">Avalokiteshvara</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Shaw_2006,_p._237_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shaw_2006,_p._237-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></i>
</p><p>Sarasvati is also briefly mentioned in the <a href="/wiki/Vajrayana" title="Vajrayana">esoteric</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Vairocan%C4%81bhisa%E1%B9%83bodhi_S%C5%ABtra" title="VairocanƒÅbhisa·πÉbodhi S≈´tra">Vairochanabhisambodhi Sutra</a></i> as one of the divinities of the western quarter of the Outer Vajra section of the <a href="/wiki/Womb_Realm" title="Womb Realm">Womb Realm Mandala</a> along with <a href="/wiki/Prithvi" title="Prithvi">Prithvi</a>, Vishnu (Narayana), <a href="/wiki/Kartikeya" title="Kartikeya">Skanda</a> (Kumara), <a href="/wiki/Vayu" title="Vayu">Vayu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chandra" title="Chandra">Chandra</a>, and their retinue. The text later also describes the <a href="/wiki/Veena" title="Veena">veena</a> as Sarasvati's symbol.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>126<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>127<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Chinese translation of this sutra renders her name variously as ËæØÊâç (Ch. <i>Bi√†nc√°i</i>; Jp. <i>Benzai</i>, lit. "eloquence"),<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>128<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> ÁæéÈü≥Â§© (Ch. <i>Mƒõiyƒ´ntiƒÅn</i>; Jp. <i>Bionten</i>, "goddess of beautiful sounds"),<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>129<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and Â¶ôÈü≥Â§© (Ch. <i>Mi√†oyƒ´ntiƒÅn</i>; Jp. <i>My≈çonten</i>, "goddess of wonderful sounds"<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>130<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>).<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>131<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Here, Sarasvati is portrayed with two arms holding a veena and situated between Narayana's consort Narayani and Skanda (shown riding on a <a href="/wiki/Peacock" class="mw-redirect" title="Peacock">peacock</a>).
</p><p>Sarasvati was initially depicted as a single goddess without consort. Her association with the bodhisattva of wisdom <a href="/wiki/Manjushri" title="Manjushri">Manjusri</a> is drawn from later tantric sources such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Yamari" title="Yamari">K·πõ·π£·πáayamƒÅri</a> tantra,</i> where she is depicted as red skinned (known as "Red Sarasvati").<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>132<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In various Indian tantric sadhanas to Sarasvati (which only survive in Tibetan translation), her <a href="/wiki/B%C4%ABja" title="Bƒ´ja">bija (seed) mantra</a> is Hrƒ´·∏•.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>133<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Nepalese_Buddhism">Nepalese Buddhism</h3></div>
<p>Sarasvati is worshiped in <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Nepal" title="Buddhism in Nepal">Nepalese Buddhism</a>, where she is a popular deity, especially for students. She is celebrated in an annual festival called Vasant Pa√±cami and children first learn the alphabet during a Sarasvati ritual.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-135"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>134<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In Nepalese Buddhism, her worship is often combined with that of <a href="/wiki/Manjushri" title="Manjushri">Manjusri</a> and many sites for the worship of Manjusri are also used to worship Sarasvati, including Svayambhu Hill.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-135"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>134<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="In_East_Asian_Buddhism">In East Asian Buddhism</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:%E9%A8%8E%E9%BE%8D%E5%BC%81%E8%B2%A1%E5%A4%A9-Benzaiten_(Goddess_of_Music_and_Good_Fortune)_Seated_on_a_White_Dragon_MET_DP135895.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img decoding="async" width="220" height="253" class="mw-file-element" 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/%E9%A8%8E%E9%BE%8D%E5%BC%81%E8%B2%A1%E5%A4%A9-Benzaiten_%28Goddess_of_Music_and_Good_Fortune%29_Seated_on_a_White_Dragon_MET_DP135895.jpg/440px-%E9%A8%8E%E9%BE%8D%E5%BC%81%E8%B2%A1%E5%A4%A9-Benzaiten_%28Goddess_of_Music_and_Good_Fortune%29_Seated_on_a_White_Dragon_MET_DP135895.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3342" data-file-height="3838" /></a><figcaption>Japanese illsutration of Benzaiten (Sarasvati) riding a dragon</figcaption></figure><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Benzaiten" title="Benzaiten">Benzaiten</a></div><p>Veneration of Sarasvati migrated from the Indian subcontinent to China with the spread of Buddhism, where she in known as Bi√†nc√°itiƒÅn (ËæØÊâçÂ§©), meaning "Eloquent Devƒ´", as well as Mi√†oyƒ´ntiƒÅn (Â¶ôÈü≥Â§©), meaning "Devƒ´ of Wonderful Sounds".<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>135<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>She is commonly enshrined in Chinese Buddhist monasteries as one of the <a href="/wiki/Twenty-Four_Protective_Deities" title="Twenty-Four Protective Deities">Twenty-Four Devas</a>, a group of protective deities who are regarded as protectors of the Buddhist dharma. Her Chinese iconography is based on her description in the <i><a href="/wiki/Golden_Light_Sutra" title="Golden Light Sutra">Golden Light Sutra</a>,</i> where she is portrayed as having eight arms, one holding a bow, one holding arrows, one holding a knife, one holding a lance, one holding an axe, one holding a pestle, one holding an iron wheel, and one holding ropes. In another popular Buddhist iconographic form, she is portrayed as sitting down and playing a <a href="/wiki/Pipa" title="Pipa">pipa</a>, a Chinese lute-like instrument.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>136<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The concept of Sarasvati migrated from India, through China to Japan, where she appears as <a href="/wiki/Benzaiten" title="Benzaiten">Benzaiten</a> (ÂºÅË≤°Â§©, lit. "<a href="/wiki/Deva_(Buddhism)" title="Deva (Buddhism)">goddess</a> of eloquence").<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>137<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Worship of Benzaiten arrived in Japan during the 6th through 8th centuries. She is often depicted holding a <i><a href="/wiki/Biwa" title="Biwa">biwa</a></i>, a traditional Japanese lute musical instrument. She is enshrined on numerous locations throughout Japan such as the Kamakura's <a href="/wiki/Zeniarai_Benzaiten_Ugafuku_Shrine" title="Zeniarai Benzaiten Ugafuku Shrine">Zeniarai Benzaiten Ugafuku Shrine</a> or Nagoya's <a href="/wiki/Kawahara_Shrine" title="Kawahara Shrine">Kawahara Shrine</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>138<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the three biggest shrines in Japan in her honour are at the <a href="/wiki/Enoshima" title="Enoshima">Enoshima</a> Island in Sagami Bay, the <a href="/wiki/Chikubu_Island" title="Chikubu Island">Chikubu Island</a> in Lake Biwa, and the <a href="/wiki/Itsukushima" title="Itsukushima">Itsukushima</a> Island in Seto Inland Sea.
</p><p>
In Japanese esoteric Buddhism (<a href="/wiki/Mikkyo" class="mw-redirect" title="Mikkyo">mikkyo</a>), the main mantra for this deity is:</p><blockquote><p><i><a href="/wiki/O%E1%B9%83" class="mw-redirect" title="O·πÉ">O·πÉ</a></i> <i>Sarasvatyai <a href="/wiki/Sv%C4%81h%C4%81" class="mw-redirect" title="SvƒÅhƒÅ">svƒÅhƒÅ</a></i> (Sino-Japanese: <i>On Sarasabatei-ei Sowaka</i>)<i>.</i><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>139<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>140<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="In_Indo-Tibetan_Buddhism">In Indo-Tibetan Buddhism</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Goddess_Sarasvati_LACMA_M.84.32.6.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/The_Goddess_Sarasvati_LACMA_M.84.32.6.jpg/220px-The_Goddess_Sarasvati_LACMA_M.84.32.6.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="318" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/The_Goddess_Sarasvati_LACMA_M.84.32.6.jpg/330px-The_Goddess_Sarasvati_LACMA_M.84.32.6.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/The_Goddess_Sarasvati_LACMA_M.84.32.6.jpg/440px-The_Goddess_Sarasvati_LACMA_M.84.32.6.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1454" data-file-height="2100" /></a><figcaption>Sarasvati in an 18th-century CE Tibetan artwork, holding a <a href="/wiki/Stick_zither" class="mw-redirect" title="Stick zither">stick zither</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Buddhism" title="Tibetan Buddhism">Indo-Tibetan Buddhism</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayan</a> regions, Sarasvati is known as <i>Yangchenma</i> (<a href="/wiki/Tibetan_script" title="Tibetan script">Tibetan</a>: <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1106655535">.mw-parser-output .uchen{font-family:"Jomolhari","Uchen","Noto Serif Tibetan Medium","Noto Serif Tibetan","BabelStone Tibetan Slim","Yagpo Tibetan Uni","Noto Sans Tibetan","Microsoft Himalaya","Kailash","DDC Uchen","TCRC Youtso Unicode","Tibetan Machine Uni","Qomolangma-Uchen Sarchen","Qomolangma-Uchen Sarchung","Qomolangma-Uchen Suring","Qomolangma-Uchen Sutung","Qomolangma-Title","Qomolangma-Subtitle","DDC Rinzin","Qomolangma-Woodblock","Qomolangma-Dunhuang"}.mw-parser-output .ume{font-family:"Qomolangma-Betsu","Qomolangma-Chuyig","Qomolangma-Drutsa","Qomolangma-Edict","Qomolangma-Tsumachu","Qomolangma-Tsuring","Qomolangma-Tsutong","TibetanSambhotaYigchung","TibetanTsugRing","TibetanYigchung"}</style><span lang="bo" class="uchen" style="font-size:1.25em; vertical-align:middle; word-wrap:break-word;">‡Ωë‡Ωñ‡æ±‡ΩÑ‡Ω¶‡ºã‡ΩÖ‡Ωì‡ºã‡Ωò</span>, <a href="/wiki/Wylie_transliteration" title="Wylie transliteration">Wylie</a>: <i>dbyangs can ma</i>, <a href="/wiki/THL_Simplified_Phonetic_Transcription" title="THL Simplified Phonetic Transcription">THL</a>: <i>yang chen ma</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>141<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which means '"Goddess of Melodious Voice". She is also called the Tara of Music (<a href="/wiki/Tibetan_script" title="Tibetan script">Tibetan</a>: <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1106655535"><span lang="bo" class="uchen" style="font-size:1.25em; vertical-align:middle; word-wrap:break-word;">‡Ωë‡Ωñ‡æ±‡ΩÑ‡Ω¶‡ºã‡ΩÖ‡Ωì‡ºã‡Ω¶‡æí‡æ≤‡Ωº‡Ω£‡ºã‡Ωò</span>, <a href="/wiki/Wylie_transliteration" title="Wylie transliteration">Wylie</a>: <i>dbyangs can sgrol ma</i>, <a href="/wiki/THL_Simplified_Phonetic_Transcription" title="THL Simplified Phonetic Transcription">THL</a>: <i>yang chen dr√∂l ma</i>) as one of the 21 <a href="/wiki/Tara_(Buddhism)" title="Tara (Buddhism)">Taras</a>. She is also considered the consort of <a href="/wiki/Manjushri" title="Manjushri">Manjushri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bodhisattva" title="Bodhisattva">bodhisattva</a> of Wisdom.<sup id="cite_ref-Sherab2007p65_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sherab2007p65-143"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>142<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>143<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Sarasvati is the divine embodiment &amp; bestower of enlightened eloquence &amp; inspiration. For all those engaged in creative endeavours in Tibetan Buddhism she is a patroness of the arts, sciences, music, language, literature, history, poetry &amp; philosophy.
</p><p>Sarasvati also became associated with the Tibetan deity <a href="/wiki/Palden_Lhamo" title="Palden Lhamo">Palden Lhamo</a> (Glorious Goddess) who is a fierce protector deity in the <a href="/wiki/Gelug" title="Gelug">Gelugpa</a> tradition known as Magzor Gyalmo (the Queen who Repels Armies).<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>144<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Sarasvati was the <a href="/wiki/Yidam" title="Yidam">yidam</a> (principal personal meditational deity) of 14th century Tibetan monk <a href="/wiki/Je_Tsongkhapa" title="Je Tsongkhapa">Je Tsongkhapa</a>, who composed a devotional poem to her.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>145<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>146<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>
Tibetan Buddhism teaches numerous mantras of Sarasvati. Her seed syllable is often Hrƒ´·πÉ.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>147<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In a <a href="/wiki/S%C4%81dhan%C4%81" title="SƒÅdhanƒÅ">sadhana</a> (ritual text) revealed by the great Tibetan female lama <a href="/wiki/Sera_Khandro" class="mw-redirect" title="Sera Khandro">Sera Khandro</a>, her mantra is presented as:<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>148<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p><blockquote><p>O·πÉ hrƒ´·πÉ devi praj√±ƒÅ vƒÅrdhani ye svƒÅhƒÅ</p></blockquote>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="In_South_East_Asian_Buddhism">In South East Asian Buddhism</h3></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Myanmar" title="Buddhism in Myanmar">Burmese Buddhism</a>, Sarasvati is worshipped as <a href="/wiki/Thurathadi" title="Thurathadi">Thurathadi</a> (<a href="/wiki/Burmese_language" title="Burmese language">Burmese</a>: <span lang="my">·Äû·Ä∞·Äõ·Äø·Äê·ÄÆ</span>), an important <a href="/wiki/Nat_(spirit)" class="mw-redirect" title="Nat (spirit)">nat</a> (Burmese deity) and is a guardian of the <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhist</a> scriptures (<a href="/wiki/Tripi%E1%B9%ADaka" class="mw-redirect" title="Tripi·π≠aka">Tipitaka</a>), scholars, students and writers.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>149<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>150<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>151<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Seekins_20062_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seekins_20062-153"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>152<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>153<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Students in Myanmar often pray for her blessings before their exams.<sup id="cite_ref-Seekins_20062_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seekins_20062-153"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>152<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 327">&#58;&#8202;327&#8202;</span></sup> She is an important deity to the esoteric <a href="/wiki/Weizza" title="Weizza">weizzas</a> (Buddhist wizards) of Burma.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>154<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>155<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In ancient <a href="/wiki/Thai_literature" title="Thai literature">Thai literature</a>, Sarasvati (<a href="/wiki/Thai_language" title="Thai language">Thai</a>: <span lang="th">‡∏™‡∏∏‡∏£‡∏±‡∏™‡∏ß‡∏î‡∏µ</span>; <small><a href="/wiki/Royal_Thai_General_System_of_Transcription" title="Royal Thai General System of Transcription">RTGS</a>:&#160;</small><span title="Royal Thai General System of Transcription"><i lang="th-Latn"><i>Suratsawadi</i></i></span>) is the goddess of speech and learning, and consort of Brahma.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>156<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Over time, Hindu and Buddhist concepts merged in Thailand. Icons of Sarasvati with other deities of India are found in old Thai <i><a href="/wiki/Wat" title="Wat">wats</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>157<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Amulets with Sarasvati and a peacock are also found in Thailand.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="In_Jainism">In Jainism</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Saraswati_Pata.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Saraswati_Pata.jpg/220px-Saraswati_Pata.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="203" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Saraswati_Pata.jpg/330px-Saraswati_Pata.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Saraswati_Pata.jpg/440px-Saraswati_Pata.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5312" data-file-height="4912" /></a><figcaption>Sarasvati Pata. The painting is divided into nine parts. In three central panels a temple enshrining Sarasvati and her <a href="/wiki/Vahana" title="Vahana">vahana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hamsa_(bird)" title="Hamsa (bird)">Hamsa</a>, are depicted. Other panels are filled with attendants, musicians, dancers and <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jain monks</a>. <a href="/wiki/Jain_art" title="Jain art">Jain style</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gujarat" title="Gujarat">Gujarat</a>, 1475‚Äì1500. <a href="/wiki/National_Museum,_New_Delhi" class="mw-redirect" title="National Museum, New Delhi">National Museum, New Delhi</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Sarasvati is also revered in <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a> as the goddess of knowledge and is regarded as the source of all learning. She is known as Srutadevata, Sarada, and Vagisvari.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>158<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Sarasvati is depicted in a standing posture with four arms, one holding a text, another holding a rosary and the remaining two holding the <a href="/wiki/Veena" title="Veena">Veena</a>. Sarasvati is seated on a lotus with the peacock as her vehicle. Sarasvati is also regarded as responsible for dissemination of <a href="/wiki/Tirthankar" class="mw-redirect" title="Tirthankar">tirthankars</a> sermon.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrasad2017192_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrasad2017192-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The earliest sculpture of Sarasvati in any religious tradition is the Mathura Jain Sarasvati from <a href="/wiki/Kankali_Tila" title="Kankali Tila">Kankali Tila</a> dating from 132&#160;CE.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelting2001_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelting2001-160"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>159<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
